<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Birth control</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Method of preventing human pregnancy</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Birth_control_(disambiguation)" title="Birth control (disambiguation)">Birth control (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="background-color: lightblue">Birth control</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg"><img alt="Package of birth control pills" data-file-height="1318" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg/280px-Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg/420px-Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg/560px-Opened_Oral_Birth_Control.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a><div class="infobox-caption">A package of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_control_pills" title="Birth control pills">birth control pills</a></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Other names</th><td class="infobox-data">Contraception, fertility control</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_Subject_Headings" title="Medical Subject Headings">MeSH</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="reflink plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="https://www.nlm.nih.gov/cgi/mesh/2016/MB_cgi?field=uid&amp;term=D003267" rel="nofollow">D003267</a></span></td></tr><tr class="noprint"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><div style="text-align: right;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q122224" title="d:Q122224">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Birth control</b>, also known as <b>contraception</b>, <b>anticonception</b>, and <b>fertility control</b>, is a method or device used to prevent <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Birth control has been used since ancient times, but effective and safe methods of birth control only became available in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup> Planning, making available, and using birth control is called <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED2012_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED2012-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-health-topic_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-health-topic-4">[4]</a></sup> Some cultures limit or discourage access to birth control because they consider it to be morally, religiously, or politically undesirable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup> The term <i>birth</i> control is a bit of a misnomer since <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> is not regularly considered under the term.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">United States Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> provide guidance on the safety of birth control methods among women with specific medical conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The most effective methods of birth control are <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(medicine)" title="Sterilization (medicine)">sterilization</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Vasectomy" title="Vasectomy">vasectomy</a> in males and <a href="/wiki/Tubal_ligation" title="Tubal ligation">tubal ligation</a> in females, <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_device" title="Intrauterine device">intrauterine devices</a> (IUDs), and <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_implant" title="Contraceptive implant">implantable birth control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011-7">[7]</a></sup> This is followed by a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraceptive" title="Hormonal contraceptive">hormone-based methods</a> including <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">oral pills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_patch" title="Contraceptive patch">patches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_ring" title="Vaginal ring">vaginal rings</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injectable_contraceptives" title="Injectable contraceptives">injections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011-7">[7]</a></sup> Less effective methods include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_contraception" title="Barrier contraception">physical barriers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condoms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diaphragm_(contraceptive)" title="Diaphragm (contraceptive)">diaphragms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_sponge" title="Contraceptive sponge">birth control sponges</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_awareness_method" title="Fertility awareness method">fertility awareness methods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011-7">[7]</a></sup> The least effective methods are <a href="/wiki/Spermicide" title="Spermicide">spermicides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Withdrawal_method" title="Withdrawal method">withdrawal by the male before ejaculation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011-7">[7]</a></sup> Sterilization, while highly effective, is not usually reversible; all other methods are reversible, most immediately upon stopping them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Safe_sex" title="Safe sex">Safe sex</a> practices, such as with the use of male or <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female condoms</a>, can also help prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infections" title="Sexually transmitted infections">sexually transmitted infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22423463_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22423463-8">[8]</a></sup> Other methods of birth control do not protect against sexually transmitted diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22341164_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22341164-9">[9]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_contraceptive" title="Emergency contraceptive">Emergency birth control</a> can prevent pregnancy if taken within 72 to 120 hours after unprotected sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gizzo2012_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gizzo2012-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Some argue <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abstinence" title="Sexual abstinence">not having sex</a> is also a form of birth control, but <a href="/wiki/Abstinence-only_sex_education" title="Abstinence-only sex education">abstinence-only sex education</a> may increase <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancies" title="Teenage pregnancies">teenage pregnancies</a> if offered without birth control education, due to non-compliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12065267_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12065267-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18923389_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18923389-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy" title="Teenage pregnancy">teenagers</a>, pregnancies are at greater risk of poor outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22764559_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22764559-14">[14]</a></sup> Comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> and access to birth control decreases the rate of unwanted pregnancies in this age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22764559_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22764559-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22764557_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22764557-15">[15]</a></sup> While all forms of birth control can generally be used by young people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p260_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p260-16">[16]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-acting_reversible_birth_control" title="Long-acting reversible birth control">long-acting reversible birth control</a> such as implants, IUDs, or vaginal rings are more successful in reducing rates of teenage pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22764557_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22764557-15">[15]</a></sup> After the delivery of a child, a woman who is not exclusively breastfeeding may become pregnant again after as few as four to six weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p260_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p260-16">[16]</a></sup> Some methods of birth control can be started immediately following the birth, while others require a delay of up to six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p260_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p260-16">[16]</a></sup> In women who are breastfeeding, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progestogen_only_contraception" title="Progestogen only contraception">progestin-only methods</a> are preferred over <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">combined oral birth control pills</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p260_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p260-16">[16]</a></sup> In women who have reached <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a>, it is recommended that birth control be continued for one year after the last period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p260_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p260-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 222 million women who want to avoid pregnancy in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a> are not using a modern birth control method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guttmacher2012_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guttmacher2012-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784540_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784540-18">[18]</a></sup> Birth control use in developing countries has decreased the number of <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">deaths during or around the time of pregnancy</a> by 40% (about 270,000 deaths prevented in 2008) and could prevent 70% if the full demand for birth control were met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784533_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784533-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784531_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784531-20">[20]</a></sup> By lengthening the time between pregnancies, birth control can improve adult women's delivery outcomes and the survival of their children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784533_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784533-19">[19]</a></sup> In the developing world, women's earnings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assets" title="Assets">assets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">weight</a>, as well as their children's schooling and health, all improve with greater access to birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784535_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784535-21">[21]</a></sup> Birth control increases <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> because of fewer dependent children, more women participating in the <a href="/wiki/Workforce" title="Workforce">workforce</a>, and less use of scarce resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784535_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784535-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784542_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784542-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_birth_control_methods" title="Comparison of birth control methods">Comparison of birth control methods</a></div>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Birth control methods include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_methods" title="Barrier methods">barrier methods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal_birth_control" title="Hormonal birth control">hormonal birth control</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrauterine_devices" title="Intrauterine devices">intrauterine devices</a> (IUDs), <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(medicine)" title="Sterilization (medicine)">sterilization</a>, and behavioral methods. They are used before or during sex while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_contraceptive" title="Emergency contraceptive">emergency contraceptives</a> are effective for up to five days after sex. Effectiveness is generally expressed as the percentage of women who become pregnant using a given method during the first year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and sometimes as a lifetime failure rate among methods with high effectiveness, such as <a href="/wiki/Tubal_ligation" title="Tubal ligation">tubal ligation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dew2012_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dew2012-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most effective methods are those that are long acting and do not require ongoing health care visits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> Surgical sterilization, implantable hormones, and intrauterine devices all have first-year failure rates of less than 1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> Hormonal contraceptive pills, patches or vaginal rings, and the <a href="/wiki/Lactational_amenorrhea" title="Lactational amenorrhea">lactational amenorrhea method</a> (LAM), if adhered to strictly, can also have first-year (or for LAM, first-6-month) failure rates of less than 1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> With typical use, first-year failure rates are considerably higher, at 9%, due to inconsistent use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> Other methods such as condoms, diaphragms, and spermicides have higher first-year failure rates even with perfect usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Pediatrics" title="American Academy of Pediatrics">American Academy of Pediatrics</a> recommends <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_acting_reversible_birth_control" title="Long acting reversible birth control">long acting reversible birth control</a> as first line for young individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>While all methods of birth control have some potential adverse effects, the risk is less than that of <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> After stopping or removing many methods of birth control, including oral contraceptives, IUDs, implants and injections, the rate of pregnancy during the subsequent year is the same as for those who used no birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>For individuals with specific health problems, certain forms of birth control may require further investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009_10_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009_10-32">[32]</a></sup> For women who are otherwise healthy, many methods of birth control should not require a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_exam" title="Medical exam">medical exam</a>—including birth control pills, injectable or implantable birth control, and condoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> For example, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelvic_exam" title="Pelvic exam">pelvic exam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breast_exam" title="Breast exam">breast exam</a>, or blood test before starting birth control pills does not appear to affect outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p10_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p10-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) published a detailed list of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_Eligibility_Criteria_for_Contraceptive_Use" title="Medical Eligibility Criteria for Contraceptive Use">medical eligibility criteria</a> for each type of birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009_10_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009_10-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hormonal">Hormonal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hormonal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraception" title="Hormonal contraception">Hormonal contraception</a> is available in a number of different forms, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_contraceptive" title="Oral contraceptive">oral pills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_implant" title="Contraceptive implant">implants</a> under the skin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injectable_contraceptives" title="Injectable contraceptives">injections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_patch" title="Contraceptive patch">patches</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal_IUD" title="Hormonal IUD">IUDs</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_ring" title="Vaginal ring">vaginal ring</a>. They are currently available only for women, although hormonal contraceptives for men have been and are being clinically tested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> There are two types of oral birth control pills, the <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">combined oral contraceptive pills</a> (which contain both <a href="/wiki/Estrogen_(medication)" title="Estrogen (medication)">estrogen</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progestin" title="Progestin">progestin</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Progestogen-only_pill" title="Progestogen-only pill">progestogen-only pills</a> (sometimes called minipills).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer_2009a_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer_2009a-38">[38]</a></sup>  If either is taken during pregnancy, they do not increase the risk of <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> nor cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_defects" title="Birth defects">birth defects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p10_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p10-35">[35]</a></sup> Both types of birth control pills prevent <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">fertilization</a> mainly by inhibiting <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">ovulation</a> and thickening cervical mucus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_2011_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson_2011-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2011_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2011-40">[40]</a></sup> They may also change the lining of the uterus and thus decrease implantation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2011_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2011-40">[40]</a></sup> Their effectiveness depends on the user's adherence to taking the pills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p10_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p10-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Combined hormonal contraceptives are associated with a slightly increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Venous_thrombosis" title="Venous thrombosis">venous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_thrombosis" title="Arterial thrombosis">arterial blood clots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review2011_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Review2011-41">[41]</a></sup> Venous clots, on average, increase from 2.8 to 9.8 per 10,000 women years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> which is still less than that associated with pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review2011_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Review2011-41">[41]</a></sup> Due to this risk, they are not recommended in women over 35 years of age who continue to smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Due to the increased risk, they are included in decision tools such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DASH_score" title="DASH score">DASH score</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PERC_rule" title="PERC rule">PERC rule</a> used to predict the risk of blood clots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The effect on sexual drive is varied, with increase or decrease in some but with no effect in most.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Combined oral contraceptives reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cancer" title="Ovarian cancer">ovarian cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Endometrial_cancer" title="Endometrial cancer">endometrial cancer</a> and do not change the risk of <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shulman2011_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shulman2011-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> They often reduce menstrual bleeding and <a href="/wiki/Dysmenorrhea" title="Dysmenorrhea">painful menstruation cramps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011p10_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011p10-35">[35]</a></sup> The lower doses of estrogen released from the vaginal ring may reduce the risk of breast tenderness, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, and headache associated with higher dose estrogen products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shulman2011_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shulman2011-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Progestin-only pills, injections and intrauterine devices are not associated with an increased risk of blood clots and may be used by women with a history of blood clots in their veins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review2011_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Review2011-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In those with a history of arterial blood clots, non-hormonal birth control or a progestin-only method other than the injectable version should be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review2011_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Review2011-41">[41]</a></sup> Progestin-only pills may improve menstrual symptoms and can be used by breastfeeding women as they do not affect <a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">milk production</a>. Irregular bleeding may occur with progestin-only methods, with some users reporting <a href="/wiki/Amenorrhea" title="Amenorrhea">no periods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21961819_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21961819-49">[49]</a></sup> The progestins <a href="/wiki/Drospirenone" title="Drospirenone">drospirenone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desogestrel" title="Desogestrel">desogestrel</a> minimize the <a href="/wiki/Androgen" title="Androgen">androgenic</a> side effects but increase the risks of blood clots and are thus not first line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The perfect use first-year failure rate of <a href="/wiki/Medroxyprogesterone_acetate" title="Medroxyprogesterone acetate">injectable progestin</a> is 0.2%; the typical use first failure rate is 6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Barrier">Barrier</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Barrier">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_contraceptive" title="Barrier contraceptive">Barrier contraceptives</a> are devices that attempt to prevent <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> by physically preventing <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> from entering the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neinstein2008_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neinstein2008-51">[51]</a></sup> They include male <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female condoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cap" title="Cervical cap">cervical caps</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diaphragm_(contraceptive)" title="Diaphragm (contraceptive)">diaphragms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_sponge" title="Contraceptive sponge">contraceptive sponges</a> with <a href="/wiki/Spermicide" title="Spermicide">spermicide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neinstein2008_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neinstein2008-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Globally, condoms are the most common method of birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male_condoms" title="Male condoms">Male condoms</a> are put on a man's erect <a href="/wiki/Human_penis" title="Human penis">penis</a> and physically block ejaculated sperm from entering the body of a sexual partner during intercourse and fellatio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamil2012_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamil2012-53">[53]</a></sup> Modern condoms are most often made from <a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">latex</a>, but some are made from other materials such as <a href="/wiki/Polyurethane" title="Polyurethane">polyurethane</a>, or lamb's intestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamil2012_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hamil2012-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">Female condoms</a> are also available, most often made of <a href="/wiki/Nitrile_rubber" title="Nitrile rubber">nitrile</a>, latex or polyurethane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Male condoms have the advantage of being inexpensive, easy to use, and have few adverse effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Making condoms available to teenagers does not appear to affect the age of onset of sexual activity or its frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In Japan, about 80% of couples who are using birth control use condoms, while in Germany this number is about 25%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebe2010_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebe2010-57">[57]</a></sup> and in the United States it is 18%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Male condoms and the diaphragm with spermicide have typical use first-year failure rates of 18% and 12%, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> With perfect use condoms are more effective with a 2% first-year failure rate versus a 6% first-year rate with the diaphragm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> Condoms have the additional benefit of helping to prevent the spread of some sexually transmitted infections such as <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, however, condoms made from animal intestine do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_FP2011_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_FP2011-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contraceptive sponges combine a barrier with a spermicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> Like diaphragms, they are inserted vaginally before intercourse and must be placed over the <a href="/wiki/Cervix" title="Cervix">cervix</a> to be effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> Typical failure rates during the first year depend on whether or not a woman has previously given birth, being 24% in those who have and 12% in those who have not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> The sponge can be inserted up to 24 hours before intercourse and must be left in place for at least six hours afterward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-6"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup> Allergic reactions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and more severe adverse effects such as <a href="/wiki/Toxic_shock_syndrome" title="Toxic shock syndrome">toxic shock syndrome</a> have been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intrauterine_devices">Intrauterine devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Intrauterine devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1019" data-file-width="2188" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg/220px-T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg/330px-T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg/440px-T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%AAte_de_st%C3%A9rilet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper T shaped IUD with removal strings</div></div></div>
<p>The current <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_device" title="Intrauterine device">intrauterine devices</a> (IUD) are small devices, often 'T'-shaped, containing either copper or <a href="/wiki/Levonorgestrel" title="Levonorgestrel">levonorgestrel</a>, which are inserted into the uterus. They are one form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-acting_reversible_contraception" title="Long-acting reversible contraception">long-acting reversible contraception</a> which are the most effective types of reversible birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Failure rates with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_IUDs" title="Copper IUDs">copper IUD</a> is about 0.8% while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levonorgestrel_intrauterine_system" title="Levonorgestrel intrauterine system">levonorgestrel IUD</a> has a failure rates of 0.2% in the first year of use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010b_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010b-63">[63]</a></sup> Among types of birth control, they, along with birth control implants, result in the greatest satisfaction among users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comm2012_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comm2012-64">[64]</a></sup> As of 2007, IUDs are the most widely used form of reversible contraception, with more than 180 million users worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darney2010_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Darney2010-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence supports effectiveness and safety in adolescents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comm2012_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Comm2012-64">[64]</a></sup> and those who have and have not previously had children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> IUDs do not affect <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a> and can be inserted immediately after delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbe2012_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbe2012-67">[67]</a></sup> They may also be used immediately after an <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Once removed, even after long term use, fertility returns to normal immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>While <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_IUDs" title="Copper IUDs">copper IUDs</a> may increase menstrual bleeding and result in more painful cramps,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grimes2007_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grimes2007-71">[71]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal_IUDs" title="Hormonal IUDs">hormonal IUDs</a> may reduce menstrual bleeding or stop menstruation altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbe2012_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbe2012-67">[67]</a></sup> Cramping can be treated with painkillers like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAIDs" title="NSAIDs">non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marnach2013_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marnach2013-72">[72]</a></sup> Other potential complications include expulsion (2–5%) and rarely perforation of the uterus (less than 0.7%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbe2012_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbe2012-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marnach2013_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marnach2013-72">[72]</a></sup> A previous model of the intrauterine device (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalkon_shield" title="Dalkon shield">Dalkon shield</a>) was associated with an increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_inflammatory_disease" title="Pelvic inflammatory disease">pelvic inflammatory disease</a>, however the risk is not affected with current models in those without <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infections" title="Sexually transmitted infections">sexually transmitted infections</a> around the time of insertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guttmacher2007_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-guttmacher2007-73">[73]</a></sup> IUDs appear to decrease the risk of <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cancer" title="Ovarian cancer">ovarian cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sterilization">Sterilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sterilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sterilisation_(medicine)" title="Sterilisation (medicine)">Surgical sterilization</a> is available in the form of <a href="/wiki/Tubal_ligation" title="Tubal ligation">tubal ligation</a> for women and <a href="/wiki/Vasectomy" title="Vasectomy">vasectomy</a> for men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup> Tubal ligation decreases the risk of <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cancer" title="Ovarian cancer">ovarian cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup> Short term complications are twenty times less likely from a vasectomy than a tubal ligation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> After a vasectomy, there may be swelling and pain of the scrotum which usually resolves in one or two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Chronic scrotal pain associated with negative impact on quality of life occurs after vasectomy in about 1-2% of men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> With tubal ligation, complications occur in 1 to 2 percent of procedures with serious complications usually due to the <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Neither method offers protection from sexually transmitted infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup> Sometimes, <a href="/wiki/Salpingectomy" title="Salpingectomy">salpingectomy</a> is also used for sterilization in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>This decision may cause regret in some men and women. Of women who have undergone tubal ligation after the age of 30, about 6% regret their decision, as compared with 20-24% of women who received sterilization within one year of delivery and before turning 30, and 6% in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nulliparous" title="Nulliparous">nulliparous</a> women sterilized before the age of 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> By contrast, less than 5% of men are likely to regret sterilization. Men who are more likely to regret sterilization are younger, have young or no children, or have an unstable marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In a survey of biological parents, 9% stated they would not have had children if they were able to do it over again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although sterilization is considered a permanent procedure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deff2011_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deff2011-83">[83]</a></sup> it is possible to attempt a <a href="/wiki/Tubal_reversal" title="Tubal reversal">tubal reversal</a> to reconnect the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallopian_tubes" title="Fallopian tubes">fallopian tubes</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Vasectomy_reversal" title="Vasectomy reversal">vasectomy reversal</a> to reconnect the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasa_deferentia" title="Vasa deferentia">vasa deferentia</a>. In women, the desire for a reversal is often associated with a change in spouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deff2011_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Deff2011-83">[83]</a></sup> Pregnancy success rates after tubal reversal are between 31 and 88 percent, with complications including an increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Ectopic_pregnancy" title="Ectopic pregnancy">ectopic pregnancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deff2011_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Deff2011-83">[83]</a></sup> The number of males who request reversal is between 2 and 6 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shri2010_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shri2010-84">[84]</a></sup> Rates of success in fathering another child after reversal are between 38 and 84 percent; with success being lower the longer the time period between the vasectomy and the reversal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shri2010_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shri2010-84">[84]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sperm_extraction" title="Sperm extraction">Sperm extraction</a> followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilization" title="In vitro fertilization">in vitro fertilization</a> may also be an option in men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral">Behavioral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Behavioral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Behavioral methods involve <a href="/wiki/Calendar-based_contraceptive_methods" title="Calendar-based contraceptive methods">regulating the timing</a> or method of intercourse to prevent introduction of sperm into the female reproductive tract, either altogether or when an egg may be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grim2004_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grim2004-86">[86]</a></sup> If used perfectly the first-year failure rate may be around 3.4%, however if used poorly first-year failure rates may approach 85%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility_awareness">Fertility awareness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fertility awareness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geburtenkontrollkette_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="a birth control chain calendar necklace" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1486" data-file-width="2318" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Geburtenkontrollkette_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Geburtenkontrollkette_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Geburtenkontrollkette_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Geburtenkontrollkette_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Geburtenkontrollkette_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Geburtenkontrollkette_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geburtenkontrollkette_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/CycleBeads" title="CycleBeads">CycleBeads</a> tool, used for estimating fertility based on days since last menstruation</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_awareness_method" title="Fertility awareness method">Fertility awareness methods</a> involve determining the most fertile days of the <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">menstrual cycle</a> and avoiding unprotected intercourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grim2004_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grim2004-86">[86]</a></sup> Techniques for determining fertility include monitoring <a href="/wiki/Basal_body_temperature" title="Basal body temperature">basal body temperature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cervical_secretions" title="Cervical secretions">cervical secretions</a>, or the day of the cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grim2004_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grim2004-86">[86]</a></sup> They have typical first-year failure rates of 24%; perfect use first-year failure rates depend on which method is used and range from 0.4% to 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> The evidence on which these estimates are based, however, is poor as the majority of people in trials stop their use early.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grim2004_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Grim2004-86">[86]</a></sup> Globally, they are used by about 3.6% of couples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sivin2010_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sivin2010-88">[88]</a></sup> If based on both basal body temperature and another primary sign, the method is referred to as symptothermal. First-year failure rates of 20% overall and 0.4% for perfect use have been reported in clinical studies of the symptothermal method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jennings_2011_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jennings_2011-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> A number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_tracking" title="Fertility tracking">fertility tracking</a> apps are available, as of 2016, but they are more commonly designed to assist those trying to get pregnant rather than prevent pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawal">Withdrawal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Withdrawal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Withdrawal_method" title="Withdrawal method">withdrawal method</a> (also known as coitus interruptus) is the practice of ending intercourse ("pulling out") before ejaculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009_100_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009_100-91">[91]</a></sup> The main risk of the withdrawal method is that the man may not perform the maneuver correctly or in a timely manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2009_100_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2009_100-91">[91]</a></sup> First-year failure rates vary from 4% with perfect usage to 22% with typical usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trus2011_23-9"><a href="#cite_note-Trus2011-23">[23]</a></sup> It is not considered birth control by some medical professionals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_29-7"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is little data regarding the sperm content of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-ejaculatory_fluid" title="Pre-ejaculatory fluid">pre-ejaculatory fluid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2009_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2009-92">[92]</a></sup> While some tentative research did not find sperm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2009_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2009-92">[92]</a></sup> one trial found sperm present in 10 out of 27 volunteers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The withdrawal method is used as birth control by about 3% of couples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sivin2010_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sivin2010-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abstinence">Abstinence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Abstinence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sexual_abstinence" title="Sexual abstinence">Sexual abstinence</a> may be used as a form of birth control, meaning either not engaging in any type of sexual activity, or specifically not engaging in vaginal intercourse, while engaging in other forms of non-vaginal sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PP2009_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-PP2009-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Complete sexual abstinence is 100% effective in preventing pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> However, among those who take a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virginity_pledge" title="Virginity pledge">pledge to abstain</a> from <a href="/wiki/Premarital_sex" title="Premarital sex">premarital sex</a>, as many as 88% who engage in sex, do so prior to marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Den2005_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Den2005-98">[98]</a></sup> The choice to abstain from sex cannot protect against pregnancy as a result of <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, and public health efforts emphasizing abstinence to reduce unwanted pregnancy may have limited effectiveness, especially in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a> and among <a href="/wiki/Disadvantaged" title="Disadvantaged">disadvantaged groups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Deliberate <a href="/wiki/Non-penetrative_sex" title="Non-penetrative sex">non-penetrative sex</a> without vaginal sex or deliberate <a href="/wiki/Oral_sex" title="Oral sex">oral sex</a> without vaginal sex are also sometimes considered birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PP2009_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-PP2009-94">[94]</a></sup> While this generally avoids pregnancy, pregnancy can still occur with <a href="/wiki/Intercrural_sex" title="Intercrural sex">intercrural sex</a> and other forms of penis-near-vagina sex (genital rubbing, and the penis exiting from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anal_intercourse" title="Anal intercourse">anal intercourse</a>) where sperm can be deposited near the entrance to the vagina and can travel along the vagina's lubricating fluids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abstinence-only_sex_education" title="Abstinence-only sex education">Abstinence-only sex education</a> does not reduce <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy" title="Teenage pregnancy">teenage pregnancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22341164_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22341164-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santelli2017_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Santelli2017-103">[103]</a></sup> Teen pregnancy rates and STI rates are generally the same or higher in states where students are given abstinence-only education, as compared with <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_sex_education" title="Comprehensive sex education">comprehensive sex education</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santelli2017_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Santelli2017-103">[103]</a></sup> Some authorities recommend that those using abstinence as a primary method have backup methods available (such as condoms or emergency contraceptive pills).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lactation">Lactation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lactation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding_and_fertility" title="Breastfeeding and fertility">Breastfeeding and fertility</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lactational_amenorrhea_method" title="Lactational amenorrhea method">lactational amenorrhea method</a> involves the use of a woman's natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postpartum_infertility" title="Postpartum infertility">postpartum infertility</a> which occurs after delivery and may be extended by <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackburn2007_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackburn2007-105">[105]</a></sup> This usually requires the presence of no <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">periods</a>, exclusively breastfeeding the infant, and a child younger than six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blenning2005_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blenning2005-26">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> states that if breastfeeding is the infant's only source of nutrition, the failure rate is 2% in the six months following delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Six uncontrolled studies of lactational amenorrhea method users found failure rates at 6 months postpartum between 0% and 7.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vander2008_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vander2008-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Updated version https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/26457821 (February 2017)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Failure rates increase to 4–7% at one year and 13% at two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritz2012_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fritz2012-108">[108]</a></sup> Feeding formula, pumping instead of nursing, the use of a <a href="/wiki/Pacifier" title="Pacifier">pacifier</a>, and feeding solids all increase its failure rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In those who are exclusively breastfeeding, about 10% begin having periods before three months and 20% before six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritz2012_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fritz2012-108">[108]</a></sup> In those who are not breastfeeding, fertility may return four weeks after delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritz2012_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fritz2012-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency">Emergency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Emergency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emergency_contraceptive_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="emergency contraceptive pills" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="807" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Emergency_contraceptive_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Emergency_contraceptive_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Emergency_contraceptive_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Emergency_contraceptive_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Emergency_contraceptive_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Emergency_contraceptive_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emergency_contraceptive_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A split dose of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_contraceptive" title="Emergency contraceptive">emergency contraceptive</a> pills</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_contraceptive" title="Emergency contraceptive">Emergency contraceptive</a> methods are medications (sometimes misleadingly referred to as "morning-after pills")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> or devices used after unprotected sexual intercourse with the hope of preventing pregnancy. Emergency contraceptives are often given to victims of <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gizzo2012_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gizzo2012-10">[10]</a></sup> They work primarily by preventing ovulation or fertilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leu2010_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leu2010-111">[111]</a></sup> They are unlikely to affect implantation, but this has not been completely excluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leu2010_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leu2010-111">[111]</a></sup> A number of options exist, including <a href="/wiki/Yuzpe_regimen" title="Yuzpe regimen">high dose birth control pills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levonorgestrel" title="Levonorgestrel">levonorgestrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mifepristone" title="Mifepristone">mifepristone</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulipristal" title="Ulipristal">ulipristal</a> and IUDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shen2019_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shen2019-112">[112]</a></sup> Providing emergency contraceptive pills to women in advance does not affect rates of sexually transmitted infections, condom use, pregnancy rates, or sexual risk-taking behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17894132_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17894132-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21923590_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21923590-114">[114]</a></sup> All methods have minimal side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shen2019_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shen2019-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Levonorgestrel" title="Levonorgestrel">Levonorgestrel</a> pills, when used within 3 days, decrease the chance of pregnancy after a single episode of unprotected sex or condom failure by 70% (resulting in a pregnancy rate of 2.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gizzo2012_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gizzo2012-10">[10]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulipristal" title="Ulipristal">Ulipristal</a>, when used within 5 days, decreases the chance of pregnancy by about 85% (pregnancy rate 1.4%) and is more effective than levonorgestrel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gizzo2012_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gizzo2012-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shen2019_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shen2019-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mifepristone" title="Mifepristone">Mifepristone</a> is also more effective than levonorgestrel, while copper IUDs are the most effective method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shen2019_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shen2019-112">[112]</a></sup> IUDs can be inserted up to five days after intercourse and prevent about 99% of pregnancies after an episode of unprotected sex (pregnancy rate of 0.1 to 0.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopkins2010_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> This makes them the most effective form of emergency contraceptive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleland2012_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cleland2012-117">[117]</a></sup> In those who are <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obese" title="Obese">obese</a>, levonorgestrel is less effective and an IUD or ulipristal is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dual_protection">Dual protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dual protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dual protection is the use of methods that prevent both <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infection" title="Sexually transmitted infection">sexually transmitted infections</a> and pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> This can be with condoms either alone or along with another birth control method or by the avoidance of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">penetrative sex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cates2002_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cates2002-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>If pregnancy is a high concern, using two methods at the same time is reasonable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cates2002_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cates2002-120">[120]</a></sup> For example, two forms of birth control are recommended in those taking the anti-<a href="/wiki/Acne" title="Acne">acne</a> drug <a href="/wiki/Isotretinoin" title="Isotretinoin">isotretinoin</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-epileptic_drugs" title="Anti-epileptic drugs">anti-epileptic drugs</a> like <a href="/wiki/Carbamazepine" title="Carbamazepine">carbamazepine</a>, due to the high risk of <a href="/wiki/Birth_defect" title="Birth defect">birth defects</a> if taken during pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta2011_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta2011-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Maternal_health" title="Maternal health">Maternal health</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg"><img alt="maternal mortality rate map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/290px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/435px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/580px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maternal mortality rate as of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA2010_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA2010-124">[124]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="675" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png/290px-Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png/435px-Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png/580px-Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scatterplot_of_contraceptive_use_and_TFR_by_region_2010.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Birth control use and <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> by region.</div></div></div>
<p>Contraceptive use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a> is estimated to have decreased the number of <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">maternal deaths</a> by 40% (about 270,000 deaths prevented in 2008) and could prevent 70% of deaths if the full demand for birth control were met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784533_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784533-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784531_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784531-20">[20]</a></sup> These benefits are achieved by reducing the number of unplanned pregnancies that subsequently result in unsafe abortions and by preventing pregnancies in those at high risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784533_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784533-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Birth control also improves child survival in the developing world by lengthening the time between pregnancies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784533_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784533-19">[19]</a></sup> In this population, outcomes are worse when a mother gets pregnant within eighteen months of a previous delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784533_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784533-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shol2010_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shol2010-125">[125]</a></sup> Delaying another pregnancy after a <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> however does not appear to alter risk and women are advised to attempt pregnancy in this situation whenever they are ready.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shol2010_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shol2010-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy" title="Teenage pregnancy">Teenage pregnancies</a>, especially among younger teens, are at greater risk of adverse outcomes including <a href="/wiki/Preterm_birth" title="Preterm birth">early birth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Low_birth_weight" title="Low birth weight">low birth weight</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">death of the infant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22764559_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22764559-14">[14]</a></sup> In 2012 in the United States 82% of pregnancies in those between the ages of 15 to 19 years old are unplanned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marnach2013_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marnach2013-72">[72]</a></sup> Comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> and access to birth control are effective in decreasing pregnancy rates in this age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Birth control methods, especially <a href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraception" title="Hormonal contraception">hormonal methods</a>, can also have undesirable side effects. Intensity of side effects can range from minor to debilitating, and varies with individual experiences. These most commonly include change in menstruation regularity and flow, nausea, breast tenderness, headaches,  weight gain, and mood changes (specifically an increase in depression and anxiety).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-128">[128]</a></sup> Additionally, hormonal contraception can contribute to bone mineral density loss,  impaired glucose metabolism, increased risk of venous thromboembolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-127">[127]</a></sup> Comprehensive sex education and transparent discussion of birth control side effects and contraindications between healthcare provider and patient is imperative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Finances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Family_economics" title="Family economics">Family economics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_raising_a_child" title="Cost of raising a child">Cost of raising a child</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="1104" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/290px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/435px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg/580px-Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Total_Fertility_Rate_Map_by_Country.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries by fertility rate (2020)</div></div></div>
<p>In the developing world, birth control increases <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> due to there being fewer dependent children and thus more women participating in or increased contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Workforce" title="Workforce">workforce</a> - as they are usually the primary <a href="/wiki/Caregiver" title="Caregiver">caregiver</a> for children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784535_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784535-21">[21]</a></sup> Women's earnings, assets, <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a>, and their children's schooling and body mass index all improve with greater access to birth control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784535_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784535-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a>, via the use of modern birth control, is one of the most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cost-effective" title="Cost-effective">cost-effective</a> health interventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsui2010_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsui2010-129">[129]</a></sup> For every dollar spent, the United Nations estimates that two to six dollars are saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22784540_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22784540-18">[18]</a></sup> These cost savings are related to preventing unplanned pregnancies and decreasing the spread of sexually transmitted illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsui2010_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsui2010-129">[129]</a></sup> While all methods are beneficial financially, the use of copper IUDs resulted in the greatest savings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsui2010_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tsui2010-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The total medical cost for a pregnancy, delivery and care of a newborn in the United States is on average $21,000 for a vaginal delivery and $31,000 for a <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean delivery</a> as of 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2013_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2013-130">[130]</a></sup> In most other countries, the cost is less than half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2013_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2013-130">[130]</a></sup> For a child born in 2011, an average US family will spend $235,000 over 17 years to raise them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDAChildExpense_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDAChildExpense-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence">Prevalence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birth_control&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Prevalence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevalence_of_birth_control" title="Prevalence of birth control">Prevalence of birth control</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg"><img alt="prevalence of modern birth control map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="854" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg/350px-SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg/525px-SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg/700px-SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SOWM2010_modern_contraception.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World map colored according to modern birth control use. Each shading level represents a range of six percentage points, with usage less than or equal to: <div> <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0;
